</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reorganization_plan_of_United_States_Army" title="Reorganization plan of United States Army">Reorganization plan of United States Army</a></div>
<p>See <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Structure of the United States Army">Structure of the United States Army</a> for detailed treatment of the <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_United_States_Army#History" title="Structure of the United States Army">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_United_States_Army#Active_and_Reserve_Components" title="Structure of the United States Army">components</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_United_States_Army#Administrative_and_Operational" title="Structure of the United States Army">administrative and operational structure</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_United_States_Army#Branches_and_Functional_Areas" title="Structure of the United States Army">branches and functional areas</a> of the Army.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1455" data-file-width="2172" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg/220px-1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg/330px-1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg/440px-1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1-175_INF_Trains_at_Fort_Dix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Army soldiers of 1st Battalion, <a href="/wiki/175th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="175th Infantry Regiment (United States)">175th Infantry Regiment</a>, Maryland Army National Guard conduct an urban cordon and search exercise as part of the army readiness and training evaluation program in the mock city of Balad at Fort Dix, New Jersey</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1955" data-file-width="3000" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg/220px-Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg/330px-Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg/440px-Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ramadi_august_2006_patrol.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. soldiers from the <a href="/wiki/6th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="6th Infantry Regiment (United States)">6th Infantry Regiment</a> taking up positions on a street corner during a foot patrol in Ramadi, Iraq</div></div></div>
<p>The U.S. Army is made up of three components: the active component, the Regular Army; and two reserve components, the Army National Guard and the Army Reserve. Both reserve components are primarily composed of part-time soldiers who train once a month – known as <a href="/wiki/Battle_Assembly" title="Battle Assembly">battle assemblies</a> or unit training assemblies (UTAs) – and conduct two to three weeks of annual training each year. Both the Regular Army and the Army Reserve are organized under <a href="/wiki/Title_10_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 10 of the United States Code">Title 10</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Code" title="United States Code">United States Code</a>, while the National Guard is organized under <a href="/wiki/Title_32_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 32 of the United States Code">Title 32</a>. While the Army National Guard is organized, trained and equipped as a component of the U.S. Army, when it is not in federal service it is under the command of individual state and territorial governors. However, the District of Columbia National Guard reports to the U.S. President, not the district's mayor, even when not federalized. Any or all of the <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_the_United_States" title="National Guard of the United States">National Guard</a> can be federalized by presidential order and against the governor's wishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. Army is led by a civilian <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">Secretary of the Army</a>, who has the statutory authority to conduct all the affairs of the army under the authority, direction and control of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Chief of Staff of the Army</a>, who is the highest-ranked military officer in the army, serves as the principal military adviser and executive agent for the Secretary of the Army, i.e., its service chief; and as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>, a body composed of the service chiefs from each of the four military services belonging to the Department of Defense who advise the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>, the Secretary of Defense and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a> on operational military matters, under the guidance of the <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vice_Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Vice Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Goldwater%E2%80%93Nichols_Act" title="Goldwater–Nichols Act">Goldwater–Nichols Act</a> mandated that operational control of the services follows a chain of command from the President to the Secretary of Defense directly to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_Combatant_Command" title="Unified Combatant Command">unified combatant commanders</a>, who have control of all armed forces units in their geographic or function area of responsibility, thus the secretaries of the military departments (and their respective service chiefs underneath them) only have the responsibility to organize, train and equip their service components. The army provides trained forces to the combatant commanders for use as directed by the Secretary of Defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg/220px-1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg/330px-1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg/440px-1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1_CAV_DIV_charge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/1st_Cavalry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Cavalry Division (United States)">1st Cavalry Division's</a> combat aviation brigade performs a mock charge with the horse detachment</div></div></div>
<p>By 2013, the army shifted to six geographical commands that align with the six geographical unified combatant commands (COCOM):
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_United_States_Army" title="Third United States Army">United States Army Central</a> headquartered at <a href="/wiki/Shaw_Air_Force_Base" title="Shaw Air Force Base">Shaw Air Force Base</a>, South Carolina</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fifth_United_States_Army" title="Fifth United States Army">United States Army North</a> headquartered at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Sam_Houston" title="Fort Sam Houston">Fort Sam Houston</a>, Texas</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_South" title="United States Army South">United States Army South</a> headquartered at Fort Sam Houston, Texas</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Europe" title="United States Army Europe">United States Army Europe</a> headquartered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clay_Kaserne" title="Clay Kaserne">Clay Kaserne</a>, Wiesbaden, Germany</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Pacific_Command" title="United States Army Pacific Command">United States Army Pacific</a> headquartered at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Shafter" title="Fort Shafter">Fort Shafter</a>, Hawaii</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Africa" title="United States Army Africa">United States Army Africa</a> headquartered at <a href="/wiki/Vicenza" title="Vicenza">Vicenza</a>, Italy</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2700" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg/220px-Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg/330px-Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg/440px-Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Army_mil-54118-2009-10-27-091030big.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Special_Forces_(United_States_Army)" title="Special Forces (United States Army)">U.S. Army Special Forces</a> soldiers from the <a href="/wiki/3rd_Special_Forces_Group_(United_States)" title="3rd Special Forces Group (United States)">3rd Special Forces Group</a> patrol a field in the Gulistan district of Farah, Afghanistan</div></div></div>
<p>The army also transformed its base unit from <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">divisions</a> to <a href="/wiki/Brigade" title="Brigade">brigades</a>. Division lineage will be retained, but the divisional headquarters will be able to command any brigade, not just brigades that carry their divisional lineage. The central part of this plan is that each brigade will be modular, i.e., all brigades of the same type will be exactly the same and thus any brigade can be commanded by any division. As specified before the 2013 end-strength re-definitions, the three major types of ground combat brigades are:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigade_Combat_Team#Heavy_brigade_combat_team" title="Brigade Combat Team">Armored</a> brigades, with strength of 4,743 troops as of 2014.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stryker_Brigade_Combat_Team" title="Stryker Brigade Combat Team">Stryker</a> brigades, with strength of 4,500 troops as of 2014.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brigade_Combat_Team#Infantry_Brigade_Combat_Team" title="Brigade Combat Team">Infantry</a> brigades, with strength of 4,413 troops as of 2014.</li></ul>
<p>In addition, there are combat support and service support modular brigades. Combat support brigades include <a href="/wiki/Combat_Aviation_Brigade" title="Combat Aviation Brigade">aviation</a> (CAB) brigades, which will come in heavy and light varieties, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fires_Brigade" title="Fires Brigade">fires</a> (artillery) brigades (now transforms to division artillery) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battlefield_Surveillance_Brigade" title="Battlefield Surveillance Brigade">expeditionary military intelligence brigades</a>. <a href="/wiki/Combat_service_support" title="Combat service support">Combat service support</a> brigades include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainment_Brigade" title="Sustainment Brigade">sustainment</a> brigades and come in several varieties and serve the standard support role in an army.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combat_maneuver_organizations">Combat maneuver organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Combat maneuver organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd><i>To track the effects of the 2018 budget cuts, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transformation_of_the_United_States_Army#Divisions_and_Brigades" title="Transformation of the United States Army">Transformation of the United States Army#Divisions and Brigades</a></i></dd></dl>
<p>The U.S. Army currently consists of 10 active divisions and one deployable division headquarters (7th Infantry Division) as well as several independent units. The force is in the process of contracting after several years of <a href="/wiki/Grow_the_Army" title="Grow the Army">growth</a>. In June 2013, the Army announced plans to downsize to 32 active combat brigade teams by 2015 to match a reduction in active duty strength to 490,000 soldiers. Army Chief of Staff Raymond Odierno projected that the Army was to shrink to "450,000 in the active component, 335,000 in the National Guard and 195,000 in U.S. Army Reserve" by 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> However, this plan was scrapped by the new administration and now the Army plans to grow by 16,000 soldiers to a total of 476,000 by October 2017. The National Guard and the Army Reserve will see a smaller expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the Army National Guard and United States Army Reserve there are a further 8 divisions, over 15 maneuver brigades, additional combat support and combat service support brigades and independent cavalry, infantry, artillery, aviation, engineer and support battalions. The Army Reserve in particular provides virtually all psychological operations and civil affairs units.
</p><p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg"><img alt="United States Army Forces Command SSI.svg" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="225" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg/25px-United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg/38px-United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg/50px-United_States_Army_Forces_Command_SSI.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Forces_Command" title="United States Army Forces Command">United States Army Forces Command</a> (FORSCOM)
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel">Personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_officer_rank_insignia" title="United States Army officer rank insignia">United States Army officer rank insignia</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_enlisted_rank_insignia" title="United States Army enlisted rank insignia">United States Army enlisted rank insignia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ranks_used_by_the_United_States_Army" title="List of ranks used by the United States Army">List of ranks used by the United States Army</a></div>
<p>These are the U.S. Army ranks authorized for use today and their equivalent NATO designations. Although no living officer currently holds the rank of <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a>, it is still authorized by Congress for use in wartime.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commissioned_officers">Commissioned officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Commissioned officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commissioned_Officers" title="Commissioned Officers">Commissioned Officers</a></div>
<p>There are several paths to becoming a commissioned officer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futuresoldiers_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-futuresoldiers-94">[94]</a></sup> including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officers' Training Corps">Reserve Officers' Training Corps</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School" title="Officer Candidate School">Officer Candidate School</a>. Regardless of which road an officer takes, the insignia are the same. Certain professions including physicians, pharmacists, nurses, lawyers and chaplains are commissioned directly into the army and are designated by insignia unique to their staff community.
</p><p>Most army commissioned officers are promoted based on an "up or out" system. The <a href="/wiki/Defense_Officer_Personnel_Management_Act" title="Defense Officer Personnel Management Act">Defense Officer Personnel Management Act</a> of 1980 establishes rules for timing of promotions and limits the number of officers that can serve at any given time.
</p><p>Army regulations call for addressing all personnel with the rank of general as "General (last name)" regardless of the number of stars. Likewise, both colonels and lieutenant colonels are addressed as "Colonel (last name)" and first and second lieutenants as "Lieutenant (last name)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar600_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-ar600-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warrant_officers">Warrant officers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Warrant officers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrant_Officer_(United_States)#Army" title="Warrant Officer (United States)">Warrant officers</a></div>
<p>Warrant officers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futuresoldiers_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-futuresoldiers-94">[94]</a></sup> are single track, specialty officers with subject matter expertise in a particular area. They are initially appointed as warrant officers (in the rank of WO1) by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Army" title="United States Secretary of the Army">Secretary of the Army</a>, but receive their <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)#Warrant_officers" title="Officer (armed forces)">commission</a> upon promotion to chief warrant officer two (CW2).
</p><p>By regulation, warrant officers are addressed as "Mr. (last name)" or "Ms. (last name)" by senior officers and as "sir" or "ma'am" by all enlisted personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar600_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-ar600-95">[95]</a></sup> However, many personnel address warrant officers as "Chief (last name)" within their units regardless of rank.
</p>
<table style="border:1px solid #8888aa; background:#f7f8ff; padding:5px; font-size:95%; margin:0 12px 12px 0;">
<tbody><tr style="background:#ccc;">
<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._uniformed_services_pay_grades#Warrant_officer_pay_grades" title="U.S. uniformed services pay grades">US DoD pay grade</a></th>

<th>W-2</th>
<th>W-3</th>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlisted_personnel">Enlisted personnel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Enlisted personnel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enlisted_rank" title="Enlisted rank">Enlisted personnel</a></div>
<p>Sergeants and corporals are referred to as NCOs, short for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Staff_Noncommissioned_Officer" title="Staff Noncommissioned Officer">non-commissioned officers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futuresoldiers_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-futuresoldiers-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-symbolsandinsig_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-symbolsandinsig-97">[97]</a></sup> This distinguishes corporals from the more numerous specialists who have the same pay grade, but do not exercise leadership responsibilities.
</p><p>Privates (E1 and E2) and privates first class (E3) are addressed as "Private (last name)", specialists as "Specialist (last name)", corporals as "Corporal (last name)" and sergeants, staff sergeants, sergeants first class and master sergeants all as "Sergeant (last name)". First sergeants are addressed as "First Sergeant (last name)" and sergeants major and command sergeants major are addressed as "Sergeant Major (last name)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar600_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-ar600-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Training">Training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="509" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg/220px-Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg/330px-Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg/440px-Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rangers_from_the_75th_Ranger_Regiment_fast-rope_from_an_MH-47_Chinook_during_a_capabilities_exercise.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rangers practice <a href="/wiki/Fast-roping" title="Fast-roping">fast roping</a> techniques from an <a href="/wiki/Boeing_CH-47_Chinook" title="Boeing CH-47 Chinook">MH-47</a> during an exercise at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Bragg" title="Fort Bragg">Fort Bragg</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="480" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg/220px-101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg/330px-101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg/440px-101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:101st-Airborne-Soldiers-build-elite-Iraqi-force-with-Ranger-Training-7-480x319.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A trainer with Company A, 1st Battalion 502nd Infantry Regiment, Task Force Strike, <a href="/wiki/101st_Airborne_Division" title="101st Airborne Division">101st Airborne Division</a> assists Iraqi army ranger students during a room clearing drill at Camp Taji, Iraq on 18 July 2016</div></div></div>
<p>Training in the U.S. Army is generally divided into two categories – individual and collective. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_training" title="Basic training">Basic training</a> consists of 10 weeks for most recruits followed by Advanced Individualized Training (AIT) where they receive training for their <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Army_careers" title="List of United States Army careers">military occupational specialties (MOS)</a>. Some individuals MOSs range anywhere from 14–20 weeks of One Station Unit Training (OSUT), which combines Basic Training and AIT. The length of AIT school varies by the MOS The length of time spent in AIT depends on the MOS of the soldier and some highly technical MOS training may require many months (e.g., foreign language translators). Depending on the needs of the army, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Basic_Training#Basic_Combat_Training" title="United States Army Basic Training">Basic Combat Training</a> for combat arms soldiers is conducted at a number of locations, but two of the longest-running are the Armor School and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Infantry_School" title="United States Army Infantry School">Infantry School</a>, both at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Benning" title="Fort Benning">Fort Benning</a>, Georgia.
</p><p>Following their basic and advanced training at the individual-level, soldiers may choose to continue their training and apply for an "additional skill identifier" (ASI). The ASI allows the army to take a wide-ranging MOS and focus it into a more specific MOS. For example, a combat medic, whose duties are to provide pre-hospital emergency treatment, may receive ASI training to become a cardiovascular specialist, a dialysis specialist or even a licensed practical nurse. For commissioned officers, training includes pre-commissioning training, known as Basic Officer Leader Course A, either at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Point" title="West Point">USMA</a> or via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ROTC" title="ROTC">ROTC</a>, or by completing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Officer_Candidate_School_(U.S._Army)" title="Officer Candidate School (U.S. Army)">OCS</a>. After commissioning, officers undergo branch specific training at the Basic Officer Leaders Course B, (formerly called Officer Basic Course), which varies in time and location according to their future assignments. Officers will continue to attend standardized training at different stages of their career.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1299" data-file-width="2048" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg/240px-Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg/360px-Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg/480px-Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yudh_Abhyas_2015_Soldiers_familiarize_with_INSAS_1B1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Army soldiers familiarizing with the latest INSAS 1B1 during exercise Yudh Abhyas 2015</div></div></div>
<p>Collective training at the unit level takes place at the unit's assigned station, but the most intensive training at higher echelons is conducted at the three combat training centers (CTC); the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Training_Center" title="National Training Center">National Training Center</a> (NTC) at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Irwin" title="Fort Irwin">Fort Irwin</a>, California, the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Polk#JRTC_moves_to_Polk" title="Fort Polk">Joint Readiness Training Center</a> (JRTC) at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Polk" title="Fort Polk">Fort Polk</a>, Louisiana and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint_Multinational_Training_Center" title="Joint Multinational Training Center">Joint Multinational Training Center</a> (JMRC) at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.hohenfels.army.mil/" rel="nofollow">Hohenfels Training Area</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hohenfels,_Bavaria" title="Hohenfels, Bavaria">Hohenfels</a>, Germany. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARFORGEN" title="ARFORGEN">ARFORGEN</a> is the Army Force Generation process approved in 2006 to meet the need to continuously replenish forces for deployment, at unit level and for other echelons as required by the mission. Individual-level replenishment still requires training at a unit level, which is conducted at the continental U.S. (CONUS) replacement center (CRC) at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Bliss" title="Fort Bliss">Fort Bliss</a>, in New Mexico and Texas before their individual deployment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_equipment_of_the_United_States_Army" title="List of equipment of the United States Army">List of equipment of the United States Army</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weapons">Weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:THAAD_Launcher.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="518" data-file-width="675" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/THAAD_Launcher.jpg/220px-THAAD_Launcher.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/THAAD_Launcher.jpg/330px-THAAD_Launcher.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/THAAD_Launcher.jpg/440px-THAAD_Launcher.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:THAAD_Launcher.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> <a href="/wiki/Terminal_High_Altitude_Area_Defense" title="Terminal High Altitude Area Defense">Terminal High Altitude Area Defense (THAAD)</a> system used by the army for ballistic missile protection</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Individual weapons</dt></dl>
<p>The army employs various individual weapons to provide light firepower at short ranges. The most common weapons used by the army are the compact variant of the <a href="/wiki/M16_rifle" title="M16 rifle">M16 rifle</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/M4_carbine" title="M4 carbine">M4 carbine</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> as well as the 7.62×51mm variant of the <a href="/wiki/FN_SCAR" title="FN SCAR">FN SCAR</a> for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Rangers" title="United States Army Rangers">Army Rangers</a>. The primary sidearm in the U.S. Army is the 9 mm <a href="/wiki/Beretta_M9" title="Beretta M9">M9 pistol</a>; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M11_pistol" title="M11 pistol">M11 pistol</a> is also used. Both handguns are to be replaced by the <a href="/wiki/SIG_Sauer_P320" title="SIG Sauer P320">M17</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modular_Handgun_System" title="Modular Handgun System">Modular Handgun System</a> program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Soldiers are also equipped with various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand_grenade" title="Hand grenade">hand grenades</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/M67_grenade" title="M67 grenade">M67 fragmentation grenade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smoke_grenade" title="Smoke grenade">M18 smoke grenade</a>.
</p><p>Many units are supplemented with a variety of specialized weapons, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M249_Squad_Automatic_Weapon" title="M249 Squad Automatic Weapon">M249 SAW</a> (Squad Automatic Weapon), to provide suppressive fire at the squad level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Indirect fire is provided by the <a href="/wiki/M320_Grenade_Launcher_Module" title="M320 Grenade Launcher Module">M320 grenade launcher</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benelli_M4_Super_90" title="Benelli M4 Super 90">M1014 Joint Service Combat Shotgun</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Mossberg_500#Model_500_vs._Model_590_vs._Model_590A1" title="Mossberg 500">Mossberg 590 Shotgun</a> are used for <a href="/wiki/Door_breaching" title="Door breaching">door breaching</a> and close-quarters combat. The <a href="/wiki/Mk_14_Enhanced_Battle_Rifle" title="Mk 14 Enhanced Battle Rifle">M14EBR</a> is used by designated marksmen. Snipers use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M82_Barrett_rifle" title="M82 Barrett rifle">M107 Long Range Sniper Rifle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/M2010_Enhanced_Sniper_Rifle" title="M2010 Enhanced Sniper Rifle">M2010 Enhanced Sniper Rifle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/M110_Semi-Automatic_Sniper_System" title="M110 Semi-Automatic Sniper System">M110 Semi-Automatic Sniper Rifle</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="1383" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg/250px-American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg/375px-American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg/500px-American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:American_troops_march_down_the_Champs_Elysees.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American troops of the 28th Infantry Division march down the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Victory_parade" title="Victory parade">victory parade</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_of_Paris" title="Liberation of Paris">Liberation of Paris</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg/220px-3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg/330px-3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg/440px-3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3rd_ID_M1A1_Abrams_TC_and_Gunner_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/3rd_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="3rd Infantry Division (United States)">3rd Infantry Division</a> soldiers manning an <a href="/wiki/M1_Abrams" title="M1 Abrams">M1A1 Abrams</a> in Iraq</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Crew served weapons</dt></dl>
<p>The army employs various crew-served weapons to provide heavy firepower at ranges exceeding that of individual weapons.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/M240_machine_gun" title="M240 machine gun">M240</a> is the U.S. Army's standard Medium Machine Gun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M2_Browning_machine_gun" title="M2 Browning machine gun">M2 heavy machine gun</a> is generally used as a vehicle-mounted machine gun. In the same way, the 40 mm <a href="/wiki/Mk_19_grenade_launcher" title="Mk 19 grenade launcher">MK 19 grenade machine gun</a> is mainly used by motorized units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. Army uses three types of <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(weapon)" title="Mortar (weapon)">mortar</a> for indirect fire support when heavier artillery may not be appropriate or available. The smallest of these is the 60 mm <a href="/wiki/M224_mortar" title="M224 mortar">M224</a>, normally assigned at the infantry company level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> At the next higher echelon, infantry battalions are typically supported by a section of 81 mm <a href="/wiki/M252_mortar" title="M252 mortar">M252 mortars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The largest mortar in the army's inventory is the 120 mm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M120_mortar" title="M120 mortar">M120/M121</a>, usually employed by mechanized units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fire support for light infantry units is provided by towed howitzers, including the 105 mm <a href="/wiki/M119_howitzer" title="M119 howitzer">M119A1</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and the 155 mm <a href="/wiki/M777_howitzer" title="M777 howitzer">M777</a> (which will replace the <a href="/wiki/M198_howitzer" title="M198 howitzer">M198</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. Army utilizes a variety of direct-fire rockets and missiles to provide infantry with an Anti-Armor Capability. The <a href="/wiki/AT4" title="AT4">AT4</a> is an unguided projectile that can destroy armor and bunkers at ranges up to 500 meters. The <a href="/wiki/FIM-92_Stinger" title="FIM-92 Stinger">FIM-92 Stinger</a> is a shoulder-launched, heat seeking anti-aircraft missile. The <a href="/wiki/FGM-148_Javelin" title="FGM-148 Javelin">FGM-148 Javelin</a> and <a href="/wiki/BGM-71_TOW" title="BGM-71 TOW">BGM-71 TOW</a> are anti-tank guided missiles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vehicles">Vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Patrol_in_Iraq,_March_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg/220px-Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg/330px-Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg/440px-Patrol_in_Iraq%2C_March_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Patrol_in_Iraq,_March_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A US soldier on patrol with the support of a <a href="/wiki/Humvee" title="Humvee">Humvee</a> vehicle</div></div></div>
<p>U.S. Army doctrine puts a premium on mechanized warfare. It fields the highest vehicle-to-soldier ratio in the world as of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>The army's most common vehicle is the High Mobility Multipurpose Wheeled Vehicle (HMMWV), commonly called the <a href="/wiki/Humvee" title="Humvee">Humvee</a>, which is capable of serving as a cargo/troop carrier, weapons platform and ambulance, among many other roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> While they operate a wide variety of combat support vehicles, one of the most common types centers on the family of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HEMTT" title="HEMTT">HEMTT</a> vehicles. The <a href="/wiki/M1_Abrams" title="M1 Abrams">M1A2 Abrams</a> is the army's <a href="/wiki/Main_battle_tank" title="Main battle tank">main battle tank</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/M2_Bradley" title="M2 Bradley">M2A3 Bradley</a> is the standard <a href="/wiki/Infantry_fighting_vehicle" title="Infantry fighting vehicle">infantry fighting vehicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Other vehicles include the <a href="/wiki/Stryker" title="Stryker">Stryker</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/M113_armored_personnel_carrier" title="M113 armored personnel carrier">M113</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armored_personnel_carrier" title="Armored personnel carrier">armored personnel carrier</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> and multiple types of <a href="/wiki/MRAP" title="MRAP">Mine Resistant Ambush Protected</a> (MRAP) vehicles.
</p><p>The U.S. Army's principal <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> weapons are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M109_Paladin" title="M109 Paladin">M109A6 Paladin</a> self-propelled howitzer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/M270_Multiple_Launch_Rocket_System" title="M270 Multiple Launch Rocket System">M270 Multiple Launch Rocket System</a> (MLRS),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> both mounted on tracked platforms and assigned to heavy mechanized units.
</p><p>While the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Aviation_Branch" title="United States Army Aviation Branch">United States Army Aviation Branch</a> operates a few <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a>, it mainly operates several types of rotary-wing aircraft. These include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AH-64_Apache" title="AH-64 Apache">AH-64 Apache</a> <a href="/wiki/Attack_helicopter" title="Attack helicopter">attack helicopter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UH-60_Black_Hawk" title="UH-60 Black Hawk">UH-60 Black Hawk</a> utility tactical transport helicopter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CH-47_Chinook" title="CH-47 Chinook">CH-47 Chinook</a> heavy-lift transport helicopter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Restructuring plans call for reduction of 750 aircraft and from 7 to 4 types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Johnson-McConnell_agreement_of_1966" title="Johnson-McConnell agreement of 1966">Johnson-McConnell agreement of 1966</a>, the Army agreed to limit its fixed-wing aviation role to administrative mission support (light unarmed aircraft which cannot operate from forward positions). For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UAV" title="UAV">UAVs</a>, the Army is deploying at least one company of drone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MQ-1C_Gray_Eagle" title="MQ-1C Gray Eagle">MQ-1C Gray Eagles</a> to each Active Army division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uniforms">Uniforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Uniforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uniforms_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Uniforms of the United States Army">Uniforms of the United States Army</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Army_Combat_Uniform" title="Army Combat Uniform">Army Combat Uniform</a> (ACU) currently features a digital Universal Camouflage Pattern (UCP) and is designed for use in woodland, desert and urban environments. However, soldiers operating in Afghanistan and trainees in Basic Combat Training (as of 2017),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> are being issued a fire-resistant ACU with the "<a href="/wiki/MultiCam" title="MultiCam">MultiCam</a>" pattern, officially known as Operation Enduring Freedom Camouflage Pattern or "OCP".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2020" data-file-width="2471" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg/220px-Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg/330px-Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg/440px-Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wayne_Downing_funeral_honor_guard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ranger Honor Platoon marching in the former service uniform</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="650" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg/220px-2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg/330px-2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg/440px-2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2010_Moscow_Victory_Day_Parade-6.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An element of the <a href="/wiki/18th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="18th Infantry Regiment (United States)">18th Infantry Regiment</a>, wearing ASUs, representing the United States at the 2010 Victory Day commemoration in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a></div></div></div>
<p>The standard garrison service uniform is the <a href="/wiki/Army_Service_Uniform" title="Army Service Uniform">Army Service Uniform</a>, which functions as both a garrison uniform (when worn with a white shirt and necktie) and a dress uniform (when worn with a white shirt and either a necktie for parades or a bow tie for after six<span class="nowrap"> </span>p.m. or black tie events).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Berets">Berets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Berets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The U.S. Army's black beret is no longer worn with the new ACU for garrison duty, having been permanently replaced with the patrol cap. After years of complaints that it was not suited well for most work conditions, Army Chief of Staff General Martin Dempsey eliminated it for wear with the ACU in June 2011. Soldiers still wear berets who are currently in a unit in jump status, whether the wearer is parachute-qualified or not (maroon beret), while members of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_Force_Assistance_Brigade_(SFAB)" title="Security Force Assistance Brigade (SFAB)">Security Force Assistance Brigades</a> (SFABs) wear brown berets. Members of the 75th Ranger Regiment and the Airborne and Ranger Training Brigade (tan beret) and Special Forces (rifle green beret) may wear it with the Army Service Uniform for non-ceremonial functions. Unit commanders may still direct the wear of patrol caps in these units in training environments or motor pools.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tents">Tents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Army&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Tents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Army has relied heavily on <a href="/wiki/Tent" title="Tent">tents</a> to provide the various facilities needed while on deployment. The most common tent uses for the military are as temporary <a href="/wiki/Barracks" title="Barracks">barracks</a> (sleeping quarters), <a href="/wiki/Mess#U.S._Army" title="Mess">DFAC</a> buildings (dining facilities),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> forward operating bases (FOBs), after action review (AAR), tactical operations center (TOC), morale, welfare and recreation (MWR) facilities, as well as security checkpoints. Furthermore, most of these tents are set up and operated through the support of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Soldier_Systems_Center" title="U.S. Army Soldier Systems Center">Natick Soldier Systems Center</a>.
</p><p>The U.S. Army is beginning to use a more modern tent called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deployable_rapid_assembly_shelter" title="Deployable rapid assembly shelter">deployable rapid assembly shelter</a> or DRASH. In 2008, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deployable_rapid_assembly_shelter" title="Deployable rapid assembly shelter">DRASH</a> became part of the Army's Standard Integrated Command Post System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SICPS_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-SICPS-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
